### [368] [Exploring the Topology and Memory of Consensus: How LLM Agents Agree, Fragment, or Settle When Forming Conventions](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04197)
*Aliakbar Mehdizadeh,Martin Hilbert*

Main category: cs.MA

TL;DR: The study explores how memory depth and network structure in multi-agent systems influence coordination and consensus, revealing that longer memory speeds up convergence in centralized networks but slows it in decentralized ones, often leading to fragmented outcomes.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: The research aims to understand the interplay between memory depth and network topology in LLM agents, focusing on how these factors affect coordination and consensus in multi-agent systems.

Method: The authors conducted 432 simulation runs of a networked Naming Game on eight fixed 16-agent topologies, varying memory depth and network structure.

Result: Longer memory decelerates steady-state achievement in decentralized networks but accelerates it in centralized ones, often resulting in fragmented opinions. Centralized networks preserve more competing conventions but settle faster with memory. Agents in clustered neighborhoods coordinate better, while high-betweenness bridges face penalties.

Conclusion: Memory depth and communication topology should be co-designed for optimal multi-agent system performance, as isolated optimization can lead to unintended outcomes like fragmented consensus.

Abstract: How much should an LLM agent remember, and how should multi-agent systems be connected when trying to reach consensus? We show these two design choices interact in a way that flips the sign of memory's effect on coordination. Across 432 simulation runs of a networked Naming Game on eight fixed 16-agent topologies, we vary memory depth and network structure. Longer memory slows the time to reach steady state in decentralized networks but accelerates it in centralized ones; the same parameter pushes the system in opposite directions depending on topology. Critically, "faster settling" in centralized networks means locking in to a fragmented plateau more quickly, not reaching system-wide consensus, which can be used to generate diverging opinions. We further document a memory-mediated speed-unity trade-off: centralized networks consistently preserve more competing conventions than decentralized networks, but their settling speed depends sharply on memory. At the agent level, within-network analyses show that high-betweenness bridges suffer a brokerage penalty while agents in locally clustered neighborhoods achieve higher coordination success. Finally, in search of analytically tractable generative mechanisms, we find that agents' choices are well captured by Fictitious Play, indicating belief-based rather than reward-based adaptation. The practical implication: memory depth and communication topology should be co-designed, not optimized in isolation.

</details>


### [369] [Organizational Control Layer: Governance Infrastructure at the Execution Boundary of LLM Agent Systems](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04306)
*Tianyu Shi,Yang Mo,Yiou Liu,Zhuonan Hao,Yin Wang,Wenzhuo Hu,Nan Yu,Meng Zhou,Jiangbo Yu*

Main category: cs.MA

TL;DR: The paper addresses the governance of LLM-based agents in workflows by proposing the Organizational Control Layer (OCL) to separate action proposal from execution, reducing unsafe actions while maintaining utility.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To solve the execution-boundary problem in LLM-based agent systems where actions must be governed before execution to prevent unsafe or non-compliant outcomes.

Method: Introduces OCL, a model-agnostic governance layer that intercepts actions before execution through policy enforcement and escalation, evaluated in adversarial negotiation environments.

Result: OCL reduces unsafe executions from 88% to near-zero and increases valid success from 12% to 96%, but reveals a tradeoff between strict governance and flexibility.

Conclusion: Deployment-grade LLM agent systems need explicit governance like OCL at the boundary between language generation and executable actions.

Abstract: LLM-based agents are increasingly deployed in workflows where generated outputs may directly trigger state-changing actions. This creates an execution-boundary problem: proposed actions must be governed before they are executed. We study this problem through economically consequential multi-agent interactions and argue that deployment-grade agent systems should separate proposal generation from environment-facing execution. To operationalize this principle, we introduce the Organizational Control Layer (OCL), a model-agnostic governance infrastructure that intercepts generated actions before execution through policy enforcement and escalation, without modifying the underlying LLM generator. We evaluate OCL on adversarial buyer--seller negotiation environments adapted from AgenticPay. Across multiple frontier LLM backends, OCL reduces unsafe executions from 88% to near-zero while increasing valid success from 12% to 96%. Results further reveal a safety--utility tradeoff: strict governance improves compliance and reliability against policy and constraint violations, but can reduce flexibility in tightly constrained markets. These findings suggest that deployment-grade LLM agent systems require explicit governance at the boundary between language generation and executable actions. The source code is available at: https://github.com/SHITIANYU-hue/amai_ocl

</details>


### [370] [Channel Fracture: Architectural Blind Spots in Scheduled Cross-Agent Memory Injection for Multi-Agent Orchestration Systems](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04896)
*Levent Liu*

Main category: cs.MA

TL;DR: The paper identifies a failure mode called 'channel fracture' in multi-agent AI orchestration systems, where scheduled agents fail to write to target agents' memory due to architectural constraints. It proposes a verification framework (CADVP v1.1) to prevent such issues.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: The study aims to address a systematic failure mode in hierarchical AI team architectures where knowledge injection between agents' persistent memory fails silently due to memory isolation guards.

Method: Experiments were conducted on a production Hermes Agent deployment with five profiles, testing three knowledge injection channels (direct SQLite writes, target-agent self-writes, and cron-delegated writes). The latter failed due to architectural constraints.

Result: Channel C (cron-delegated writes) failed completely due to hardcoded memory isolation and bypassed initialization in cron contexts. The proposed CADVP v1.1 framework includes a verification check to prevent false positives.

Conclusion: The paper introduces CADVP v1.1 to ensure reliable cross-agent memory delivery and proposes two design principles (inverse verification and channel matching) for robust architectures.

Abstract: Multi-agent AI orchestration systems increasingly rely on persistent memory to maintain context across sessions, agents, and tasks. When one agent must inject knowledge into another agent's memory -- a common requirement in hierarchical team architectures -- the delivery mechanism must be architecturally sound. We report the discovery of a systematic failure mode we term channel fracture: a condition where scheduled (cron) agents in orchestration frameworks are silently unable to write to the target agent's persistent memory due to hardcoded memory isolation guards. Through experiments on a production Hermes Agent deployment with five specialized profiles, we tested three injection channels: (A) direct SQLite database writes, (B) target-agent self-writes via memory tools, and (C) cron-delegated writes. Channel C failed completely due to two architectural constraints: skip_memory=True hardcoded at the scheduler layer and dynamic registration of memory tools contingent on _memory_manager initialization, which is bypassed in cron execution contexts. We propose CADVP (Cross-Agent Delivery Verification Protocol) v1.1, a 13-dimension verification framework with a veto-level channel confirmation check (CC-0) that prevents false-positive delivery assurance. We articulate two design principles: the inverse verification principle and the channel matching principle.

</details>
